Lapse of Maintenance If Not Drawn After Judge's Order
Ruling on maintenance arrears dropping if not taken for a period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lapse of past-due maintenance and identify the exception that preserves the debt.

Core Idea
Past-due maintenance lapses automatically unless a judge authorized borrowing to preserve the debt.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling: when a judge orders maintenance for dependents, and the beneficiary does not take it for a period, the arrears are dropped. The exception occurs if the judge permitted borrowing on behalf of the obligated person. The underlying principle is that nafaqa is a gift (silah) based on need, not a wage; thus, it does not accumulate as a debt unless special authorization is given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? A judge's maintenance order for child, parents, and relatives. The ruling: if a period passes without the beneficiary taking the maintenance, it lapses.
2
State the exception
The ruling has one exception: if the judge authorized the beneficiary to borrow against the maintenance (istidanah), the arrears become a debt and do not lapse.
3
Explain the underlying principle
The text provides a principle: nafaqa is a silah (gift/connection) that becomes obligatory due to need. It is not a wage (muzara'ah). Therefore, if the beneficiary does not take it, the obligation for that period drops because the need was not actualized.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider nafaqa as a debt akin to wages, which would persist. The Hanafi view is based on the nature of nafaqa as a renewable need-based entitlement.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a wife awarded monthly maintenance by a judge. She does not claim it for six months. Under Hanafi law, she loses those six months' arrears unless the judge had permitted her to borrow on the husband's behalf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قَضَى الْقَاضِي لِلْوَلَدِ وَالْوَالِدَيْنِ وَذَوِي الْأَرْحَامِ بِالنَّفَقَةِ فَمَضَتْ مُدَّةٌ سَقَطَتْ إِلَّا أَنْ يَأْذَنَ الْقَاضِي فِي الِاسْتِدَانَةِ عَلَيْهِ. أَصْلٌ: نَفَقَةٌ صِلَةٌ وَهُوَ بِالِاحْتِيَاجِ لَازِمٌ، لَيْسَتْ بِمُزَارَعَةٍ، فَلَوْ تَرَكَ الْأَخْذَ مُدَّةً سَقَطَتْ.
And when the judge orders maintenance for the child, parents, and relatives, and a period passes (without taking it), it drops, unless the judge authorized borrowing against it. Principle: Nafaqa is a gift (silah) and becomes obligatory due to need; it is not a wage. So if one leaves taking it for a period, it drops.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This ruling deals with maintenance arrears. If a judge has ordered maintenance for dependents (child, parents, relatives) and the beneficiary does not take it for a period, the right to that past maintenance is dropped (سقطت). The exception: if the judge had authorized the beneficiary to borrow on behalf of the obligated person (استدانة), then the debt remains. The underlying principle (أصل) is that nafaqa is a form of gift (silah) linked to need; if the need is not actualized by taking, the obligation lapses. Unlike wages, nafaqa does not accumulate as a debt automatically. This encourages prompt fulfillment and prevents accumulation of claims.

Key Terms
استدانة (istidanah): Borrowing on someone's behalf, often with judicial permission.
صلة (silah): Gift or charitable connection, not a contractual exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does past-due maintenance remain enforceable after a period?
A) If the beneficiary is poor
B) If the judge authorized borrowing
C) If the obligated person is absent
D) It never drops

Key Takeaway
Past-due maintenance lapses automatically in Hanafi law unless the judge authorized borrowing to preserve the debt.

'Aqila of a Freed Slave and Ally
Ruling with proofs on tribal liability for blood money

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'aqila of a freed slave and a mawla al-muwalat, and explain the proofs from the Sunnah and sayings of the early generations.

Core Idea
The patron's tribe is the 'aqila for a freed slave and an ally — the mawla is like family.

In Hanafi fiqh, the 'aqila (tribal liability for blood money) is established by the practice of the Prophet and the Companions. This rule from al-Quduri specifies who bears that responsibility for a freed slave (mu'taq) and for a person who enters into an alliance (mawla al-muwalat). Understanding this ruling clarifies how liability is distributed in accidental killing cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage gives two rulings: (1) The 'aqila of a freed slave is the tribe of his patron. (2) The 'aqila of a mawla al-muwalat is his patron and the patron's tribe.
Note the difference: for a freed slave, the patron's whole tribe is liable; for an ally, the patron and his tribe share liability.
2
Examine the primary proof (hadith)
The author cites the hadith 'Mawla al-qawm min anfusihim' from Abu Rafi'. This hadith indicates that the mawla is considered part of the family, so the patron's tribe stands as his 'aqila.
The hadith also mentions that sadaqah is not permissible for the Prophet's household, showing that the mawla shares the same rulings as the family.
3
Consider the supplementary proofs
The saying of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i confirms that the mawla al-muwalat's blood money and inheritance go to the patron. The saying of Tamim al-Dari states the patron is the most entitled to the freed slave in life and death, implying liability.
These strengthen the ruling by showing early practice (amal) of the salaf.
4
Cross-reference the Hanafi position
This ruling is part of Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position. There is mild ikhtilaf from other schools that may prioritize blood ties over clientage.
The Hanafi school relies on the explicit hadith and the practice of the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعَاقِلَةُ الْمُعْتَقِ قَبِيلَةُ مَوْلاهُ وَمَوْلَى الْمُوَالَاةِ يَعْقِلُ عَنْهُ مَوْلاهُ وَقَبِيلَتُهُ
وجه (۱) الحديث لثبوت وَعَاقِلَةُ الْمُعْتَقِ قَبِيلَةُ مَوْلَاهُ عَنِ ابْنِ أَبي رَافِعٍ، عَنْ أَبِي رَافِعٍ، أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ بَعَثَ رَجُلًا عَلَى الصَّدَقَةِ مِنْ بَنِي مَخْزُومٍ، فَقَالَ لِأَبِي رَافِعِ اصْحَبْنِي فَإِنَّكَ تُصِيبُ مِنْهَا، قَالَ: حَتَّى آتي النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَأَسْأَلَهُ، فَأَتَاهُ فَسَأَلَهُ، فَقَالَ: «مَوْلَى الْقَوْمِ مِنْ أَنْفُسِهِمْ، وَإِنَّا لَا تَحِلُّ لَنَا الصَّدَقَةُ»
وجه (۲) قول التابعى لثبوت وَعَاقِلَةُ الْمُعْتَقِ قَبِيلَةُ مَوْلَاهُ عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ فِي الرَّجُلِ يُوَالِي الرَّجُلَ فَيُسْلِمُ عَلَى يَدَيْهِ قَالَ: «يَعْقِلُ عَنْهُ وَيَرِثُهُ»
وجه : (۳) قول الصحابي لثبوت وَعَاقِلَةُ الْمُعْتَقِ قَبِيلَةُ مَوْلاهُ عَنْ تَمِيمِ الدَّارِيِّ رَفَعَهُ قَالَ هُوَ أَوْلَى النَّاسِ بِمَحْيَاهُ وَمَمَاتِهِ
The 'aqila of a freed slave is the tribe of his patron. And the mawla al-muwalat (ally), his patron and his tribe are liable for his 'aql (blood money).
Proof (1): Hadith for the ruling: The 'aqila of a freed slave is the tribe of his patron. From Ibn Abi Rafi', from Abu Rafi', that the Prophet sent a man from Banu Makhzum to collect sadaqah. He said to Abu Rafi', "Accompany me, you will get some of it." He said, "Let me go to the Prophet and ask him." So he went and asked, and he said, "The mawla of a people is from among themselves, and sadaqah is not permissible for us."
Proof (2): Saying of a Tabi'i for the ruling: From Ibrahim regarding a man who makes a pact of alliance (muwalah) with another man and becomes Muslim at his hands. He said, "He is responsible for his blood money and inherits from him."
Proof (3): Saying of a Sahabi for the ruling: From Tamim al-Dari, raising it (to the Prophet), he said: "He is the most entitled of people to his life and death."
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs from Al-Quduri's matn on 'aqila. The ruling states that the 'aqila (the clan liable for blood money) of a freed slave (mu'taq) is the tribe of his patron (mawla). Similarly, a person who enters into a pact of alliance (mawla al-muwalat) has his 'aqila liability borne by his patron and the patron's tribe. The author then provides three proofs: (1) a hadith from Abu Dawud and Bukhari where the Prophet says 'the mawla of a people is from among themselves,' establishing that the patron's tribe is like the freed slave's own family; (2) the saying of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, a Tabi'i, that such a mawla is liable for blood money and inherits; (3) the saying of Tamim al-Dari, a Sahabi, that the patron is the closest person to the freed slave in life and death, implying liability for blood money. The Hanafi position is that the 'aqila of the patron's tribe is responsible, not the state or the individual alone.

Key Terms
عَاقِلَة ('āqila): The clan or group of male relatives who are obligated to pay blood money (diyah) on behalf of a member who commits accidental killing.
مَوْلَى الْمُوَالَاة (mawla al-muwalāt): A person who enters into an alliance (muwalah) with another, becoming a client for purposes of inheritance and blood money liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is liable for the blood money of a freed slave (mu'taq) in case of accidental killing?
A) The freed slave himself from his own wealth
B) The tribe of the patron who freed him
C) The state treasury (Bayt al-Mal)
D) The freed slave's former master individually

Key Takeaway
The patron's tribe is the 'aqila for a freed slave and a mawla al-muwalat, based on the hadith 'Mawla al-qawm min anfusihim' and the practice of the early generations.

Minimum Amount for 'Aqila Liability
Ruling with proofs for the minimum 'aqila liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum amount for which the 'aqila is liable and explain the proof from the hadith of the two women of Hudhayl.

Core Idea
The 'aqila bears only half a tenth of the diyah or more; less is on the offender.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi criminal law: the minimum threshold for the 'aqila's liability. The 'aqila, a group of male relatives, is responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental killing. However, they are not liable for trivial amounts. This passage from al-Quduri establishes that the 'aqila only bears half a tenth (nisftu 'ushr) of the full diyah or more. The ruling is supported by the hadith of the two women of Hudhayl and the determination of the ghurrah's value.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its legal force
Identify the act (aqila liability) and the hukm level (wajib). Note the specific threshold: half a tenth of the full diyah and above.
This is a wajib obligation on the 'aqila, not a mere recommendation.
2
Analyze the first proof: the hadith
Read the hadith from Abu Hurayrah. Identify that the Prophet placed the woman's diyah on her 'aqila and the fetus's diyah (ghurrah) was set at half a tenth. This shows the minimum the 'aqila must bear is at least the value of a ghurrah.
The ghurrah is worth half a tenth of the full diyah.
3
Confirm with the second proof: sayings of the Tabi'un
The quotations from al-Sha'bi and Rabi'ah fix the value of the ghurrah as 500 dirhams or 50 dinars, reinforcing the threshold.
This is a companion-level transmission supporting the ruling.
4
Extract the usul principle
The Urdu usul note clarifies that if the diyah is less than one-twentieth of the full diyah, the killer pays from his own wealth. This is the operative rule derived from the proofs.
The threshold is clearly defined to prevent trivial burdens on the 'aqila.
5
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Consider a case where diyah is assessed at 300 dirhams versus 600 dirhams. Determine who pays: the offender for the first, the 'aqila for the second.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا تَتَحَمَّلُ الْعَاقِلَةُ أَقَلَّ مِنْ نِصْفِ عُشْرِ الدِّيَةِ وَتَتَحَمَّلُ نِصْفَ الْعُشْرِ فَصَاعِدًا وَمَا نَقَصَ مِنْ ذَلِكَ فَفِي مَالِ الْجَانِي
وجه: (ا) الحديث لثبوت وَلَا تَتَحَمَّلُ الْعَاقِلَةُ أَقَلَّ مِنْ نِصْفِ عُشْرِ الدِّيَةِ أَنَّ أَبَا هُرَيْرَةَ قَالَ: «اقْتَتَلَتِ امْرَأَتَانِ مِنْ هُدَيْلِ، فَرَمَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا الْأُخْرَى بِحَجَرٍ قَتَلَتْهَا وَمَا فِي بَطْنِهَا، فَاخْتَصَمُوا إِلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَضَى أَنَّ دِيَةَ جَنِينِهَا غُرَّةً، عَبْدٌ أَوْ وَلِيدَةٌ، وَقَضَى دِيَةَ الْمَرْأَةِ عَلَى عَاقِلَتِهَا»
وجه (۲) قول التابعى لثبوت وَلَا تَتَحَمَّلُ الْعَاقِلَةُ أَقَلَّ مِنْ نِصْفِ عُشْرِ الدِّيَةِ عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ، وَجَابِرٍ، عَنِ الشَّعْبِي، قَالَ: «الْغُرَّةُ خَمْسُ مِائَةِ دِرْهَم» قَالَ أَبُو دَاوُدَ: قَالَ رَبِيعَةُ: " الْغُرَّةُ: خَمْسُونَ دِينَارًا "
اصول: پوری دیت بیسواں حصہ سے کم دیت ہو تو وہ قاتل ادا کرئیں گے، اس کے عاقلہ نہیں۔
The 'aqila is not liable for less than half a tenth of the full diyah. It is liable for half a tenth and above. What is less than that is in the wealth of the offender.
Proof (1): Hadith for the ruling: Abu Hurayrah said: "Two women from Hudhayl fought, and one threw a stone at the other, killing her and what was in her womb. They brought the case to the Prophet, and he ruled that the diyah of her fetus is a ghurrah (a slave or slave-girl), and he ruled that the diyah of the woman is upon her 'aqila."
Proof (2): Saying of a Tabi'i: From Ibrahim, Jabir, and al-Sha'bi who said: "The ghurrah is five hundred dirhams." Abu Dawud said: Rabi'ah said: "The ghurrah is fifty dinars."
Principle (Usul): If the diyah is less than one-twentieth of the full diyah, the killer will pay it; not his 'aqila.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and its proofs regarding the minimum amount for which the 'aqila is liable. The 'aqila does not bear liability for amounts less than half a tenth (nisftu 'ushr) of the full diyah. Half a tenth is 1/20 of the full diyah, which is 50 dinars or 500 dirhams (full diyah being 1000 dinars or 10,000 dirhams in the Hanafi school). If the amount is less, the offender pays from his own property. The hadith from Abu Hurayrah shows that for a fetus, the diyah is a ghurrah, which is worth half a tenth, and this was placed on the 'aqila. This demonstrates the minimum. The saying of the Tabi'i and the subsequent report confirm the value of the ghurrah. The usul note in Urdu reiterates: if the diyah is less than a twentieth, the killer pays it himself. This ruling ensures that the 'aqila only bears significant amounts, not trivial ones.

Key Terms
عُشْرُ الدِّيَة ('ushr al-diyah): A tenth of the full diyah; half of that (nisftu 'ushr) is the minimum liability of the 'aqila.
غُرَّة (ghurrah): A compensatory payment for a fetus, equivalent to a slave or slave-girl, valued at half a tenth of the full diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For what amount does the 'aqila become liable?
A) Any amount, no matter how small
B) Exactly half a tenth of the diyah
C) Half a tenth or more
D) More than half a tenth only

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila is only liable for half a tenth of the diyah or more; any lesser amount is the offender's personal responsibility.

'Aqila Not Liable for Slave's Crime
Ruling with proof from Ibn 'Abbas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of the 'aqila for a slave's crime and identify the dalil (saying of Ibn 'Abbas) used as proof.

Core Idea
The 'aqila is not liable for a slave's crime—the master bears the responsibility.

The 'aqila—the clan or group responsible for paying blood money—plays a central role in Hanafi tort law. However, its liability is not unlimited. This lesson examines a clear boundary: crimes committed by a slave. Understanding this ruling sharpens your grasp of the scope of 'aqila liability and the principle that a slave, as property, does not shift his torts onto the community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that the 'aqila does not bear liability for a crime (jinayah) committed by a slave. The act is a crime by a slave, and the ruling is that the 'aqila is exempt.
The ruling is negative: the 'aqila is not liable.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a saying of the companion Ibn 'Abbas (ra) as proof. Find the exact wording: 'La ta'qil al-'aqilat 'amdan wa la sudhan wa la i'tirafan wa la ma jana al-mamluk.'
The dalil is a qawl sahabi, which is a source in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'wa la ma jana al-mamluk' (nor for what a slave commits) explicitly excludes slave crimes from 'aqila liability. This is a direct textual indication.
The exclusion is unambiguous (zahir al-dalalah).
4
Note the school's position
The Hanafi school adopts this ruling as the mufta bihi position. It is consistent with the principle that the slave's value and his master bear the consequences of his torts.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded on this specific point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا تَعْقِلُ الْعَاقِلَةُ جِنَايَةَ الْعَبْدِ
حوجه: (ا) قول الصحابي لثبوت وَلَا تَعْقِلُ الْعَاقِلَةُ جِنَايَةَ الْعَبْدِ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ قَالَ: لَا تَعْقِلُ الْعَاقِلَةُ عَمْدًا وَلَا صُدْحًا وَلَا اعْتِرَافًا وَلَا مَا جَنَى الْمَمْلُوكُ
The 'aqila is not liable for the crime of a slave.
Proof: Saying of a Sahabi: From Ibn 'Abbas, he said: "The 'aqila is not liable for intentional killing, nor for a 'abd (slave?), nor for acceptance, nor for what a slave commits."
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This ruling states that the 'aqila is not responsible for crimes (jīnāyah) committed by a slave. The proof is a saying of Ibn 'Abbas which lists several cases where the 'aqila is not liable: intentional acts ('amd), certain injuries (sudh), confessions, and what a slave does. The slave is considered property, and his crimes are covered by his value or by his master, not by the 'aqila. The Hanafi school maintains this position, with the exception that if the slave is killed, the liability is on the killer's 'aqila (as in a later lesson).

Key Terms
جِنَايَة (jināyah): An act causing harm that may require compensation, including accidental killing or injury.
عَاقِلَة (ʿāqila): The group of male relatives (or tribe/community) who collectively pay blood money (diyah) on behalf of a member who commits accidental homicide or certain injuries.
مَمْلُوك (mamlūk): A slave; a human being owned as property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the ruling in this lesson, who bears the liability for a crime committed by a slave?
A) The 'aqila of the slave
B) The master of the slave
C) The 'aqila of the victim
D) The slave himself

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila is not liable for a slave's crime; the master bears the financial responsibility.

Confession Not Binding on 'Aqila Without Confirmation
Ruling that the 'aqila is not bound by the offender's confession unless they confirm it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the 'aqila is not bound by an offender's confession unless they confirm it, and apply it to a scenario involving disputed liability.

Core Idea
Confession alone does not bind the 'aqila — only their confirmation makes them liable.

In Hanafi fiqh, the 'aqila (the offender's clan or group responsible for paying diyah) is not automatically liable when an offender confesses to a crime requiring compensation. This lesson examines the ruling from Al-Quduri and the proof from the practice of Madinan jurists, which protects the 'aqila from being burdened by an unconfirmed confession. The underlying principle is that mere confession does not determine diyah; the amount is settled by the qadi's judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the ruling exactly as given in the matn: the 'aqila does not bear the crime confessed to unless they confirm the offender.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the statement of the Madinan jurists transmitted by Ibn Abi al-Zinad, listing cases where 'aqila is not liable, including 'i'tiraf.
This is a qawl tabi'i, not a direct hadith.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit inclusion of 'confession' in the list shows that confession alone does not obligate the 'aqila; they must confirm it for liability.
The phrase 'illa an yuhibbu dhalika tawlan minhum' indicates voluntary acceptance is an exception.
4
Note the exceptions
The 'aqila may voluntarily accept liability even for these categories, but it is not imposed.
The ruling protects the 'aqila from unilateral burden.
5
Apply the usul principle
The diyah amount is not fixed by confession; it requires the qadi's judgment to determine based on evidence.
This prevents the offender from dictating the amount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا تُعْقَلُ الْجُنَايَةُ الَّتِي اعْتَرَفَ بِمَا الْجَانِي إِلَّا أَنْ يُصَدِّقُوهُ
(۱)قول التابعي لثبوت وَلَا تُعْقَلُ الْحِنَايَةُ الَّتِي اعْتَرَفَ بِمَا الْجَانِي عَنِ ابْنِ أَبِي أُوَيْسٍ، وَعِيسَى بْنُ مِينَا، قَالَا: ثنا ابْنُ أَبِي الزِّنَادِ، عَنْ أَبِيهِ، عَنِ الْفُقَهَاءِ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْمَدِينَةِ كَانُوا يَقُولُونَ: لَا تَحْمِلُ الْعَاقِلَةُ مَا كَانَ عَمْدًا، وَلَا بِصُلْحٍ، وَلَا اعْتِرَافٍ، وَلَا مَا جَنَى الْمَمْلُوكُ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُحِبُّوا ذَلِكَ طَوْلًا مِنْهُمْ
اصول: صرف اقرار سے دیت کا تعین نہیں ہو گا، بلکہ قضاء قاضی کے بعد سے دیت کا تعین ہو گا۔
The crime which the offender confesses to is not borne (by the 'aqila) unless they confirm him.
Proof (1): Saying of a Tabi'i: From Ibn Abi Uways and 'Isa ibn Mina, they said: Ibn Abi al-Zinad narrated to us from his father, from the jurists of Madinah, they used to say: "The 'aqila does not bear what was intentional, nor (an amount fixed by) compromise, nor a confession, nor what a slave committed, unless they accept that voluntarily."
Principle (Usul): Mere confession will not determine the diyah; rather the diyah is determined after the qadi's judgment.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that the 'aqila is not automatically liable for a crime simply because the offender confessed to it. The 'aqila members must confirm (saddaqu) the confession for it to be binding on them. The proof comes from the practice of the Madinan jurists, who also listed other cases where the 'aqila is not liable: intentional acts, amounts fixed by sulh (compromise), confessions, and slave crimes, unless they voluntarily accept. The usul note in Urdu reinforces that the diyah is not fixed by mere confession; it requires a qadi's judgment. This prevents an offender from burdening his clan through a unilateral confession.

Key Terms
اعْتِرَاف (i'tirāf): A confession of guilt by the offender regarding a crime that would normally require compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the 'aqila become liable due to a confession?
A) Whenever the offender confesses
B) Only if the 'aqila members confirm the confession
C) If the qadi accepts the confession
D) Never, regardless of confirmation

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila is only liable if they confirm the confession; confession alone does not bind them.

Delayed Confession of Accidental Killing: Diyah from Offender's Wealth Over Three Years
Ruling on payment of diyah when confession occurs long after the incident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a person confesses to accidental killing after a long delay, and identify the conditions under which the diyah is paid from the offender's own wealth in three annual installments.

Core Idea
Delay in confession shifts diyah from the 'aqila to the offender, payable in three years from the judgment.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a person admits to an accidental killing long after the incident, and the case is only brought to the qadi years later. Normally, the 'aqila would bear the diyah, but due to the delay and the lack of confirmation from the 'aqila, the liability falls on the confessing offender. The qadi decrees the payment of diyah from the offender's own wealth, spread over three years from the date of the judgment. This ruling ensures justice while providing manageable payment terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is confessing to accidental killing after a long delay. The ruling is that diyah becomes wajib (obligatory) on the offender's own wealth, not on the 'aqila.
The ruling applies only when the confession occurs after years and the case has not been brought to the qadi earlier.
2
List the conditions for this ruling
Two conditions: (1) the killing is accidental (khaṭaʾ), (2) the confession occurs after a long delay (years) without prior court involvement.
The delay is the key factor that changes the default liability from the 'aqila to the offender.
3
Determine the payment method and timeline
The diyah is to be paid in three annual installments, starting from the date of the qadi's decree, not from the date of the crime.
The phrase 'fi thalathi sinin min yawmi yuqda 'alayhi' specifies this timeline.
4
Contrast with the normal rule of 'aqila liability
Normally, in accidental killing, the diyah is on the 'aqila over three years. Here, due to the delay and lack of confirmation, it shifts to the offender alone.
This exception is specific to delayed confession without earlier legal proceedings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِقَتْلِ خَطَا وَلَمْ يَرْتَفِعُوا إِلَى الْقَاضِي إِلَّا بَعْدَ سِنِينَ قُضِيَ عَلَيْهِ بِالدِّيَةِ فِي مَالِهِ فِي ثَلَاثِ سِنِينَ مِنْ يَوْمِ يُقْضَى عَلَيْهِ
اصول: اور فیصلہ کے بعد تین سال تک قاتل کے مال سے دیت لی جائے گی۔
And whoever admits to accidental killing, and they do not come to the qadi except after years, the diyah is decreed against him from his own wealth, payable in three years from the day it is decreed.
Principle (Usul): And after the judgment, the diyah will be taken from the killer's wealth over three years.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This ruling handles a scenario where a person confesses to a killing long after the event. Normally, the 'aqila would pay the diyah over three years, but here, due to the delay and because the 'aqila did not confirm the confession (as per the previous ruling), the liability falls entirely on the offender. The payment is spread over three years from the date of the qadi's ruling, not from the date of the crime. The usul note emphasizes this three-year payment period from the killer's own wealth. This ensures the diyah is collected in manageable installments.

Key Terms
خَطَا (khaṭaʾ): Accidental killing, as opposed to intentional ('amd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Delay in confession shifts diyah from the 'aqila to the offender, payable in three years from the judgment date.

The 'Aqila's Non-Liability for Sulh Amounts
Ruling that the 'aqila is not liable for amounts fixed by sulh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the 'aqila is not bound by a sulh agreement and identify the proof from the practice of Madinan jurists.

Core Idea
The 'aqila is not liable for any amount fixed by sulh unless they voluntarily consent.

This lesson addresses a specific protection for the 'aqila (the paternal relatives who pay blood money). The text establishes that when the offender and the victim's family reach a compromise (sulh) on compensation, the 'aqila is not automatically liable for that amount. The ruling prevents the 'aqila from being bound by private settlements they never agreed to, ensuring they are only responsible for the standard diyah in accidental cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the 'aqila's liability (or non-liability) for a compensation amount that was agreed through sulh between the offender and the victim's family. The ruling: the 'aqila is NOT liable for that amount.
The ruling is negative – it removes an obligation, not imposes one.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the statement of the Madinan jurists (Tabi'i) recorded by al-Bayhaqi: "The 'aqila does not bear what was intentional, nor (an amount fixed by) compromise, nor a confession, nor what a slave committed, unless they accept that voluntarily."
This is the same proof used for the previous ruling on confession.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit phrase 'bi sulh' (by compromise) is listed among the exceptions. This shows that sulh amounts are not automatically binding on the 'aqila. The wording directly supports the ruling.
The proof is zanni in both transmission and meaning, but sufficient for this ruling.
4
Note the exception
The statement concludes 'illa an yuhibbu dhalika tawlan minhum' – unless they accept that voluntarily. This means if the 'aqila freely chooses to pay the sulh amount, they become obligated. The exception preserves their right to consent.
This exception is crucial – it shows the default is non-liability, but consent overrides it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا تَعْقِلُ مُلْزَمًا بِالصُّلْحِ
الوجه: (1)قول التابعى لثبوت وَلَا تَعْقِلُ مُلْزَمًا بِالصُّلْحِ ثنا ابْنُ أَبِي أُوَيْسٍ، وَعِيسَى بْنُ مِينَا، قَالا : ثنا ابْنُ أَبِي الزِّنَادِ، عَنْ أَبِيهِ، عَنِ الْفُقَهَاءِ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْمَدِينَةِ كَانُوا يَقُولُونَ: لَا تَحْمِلُ الْعَاقِلَةُ مَا كَانَ عَمْدًا، وَلَا بِصُلْحِ، وَلَا اعْتِرَافٍ وَلَا مَا جَنَى الْمَمْلُوكُ إِلَّا أَنْ يُحِبُّوا ذَلِكَ طَوْلًا مِنْهُمْ
And (the 'aqila) is not liable for a binding (amount from) sulh (compromise).
Proof: Saying of a Tabi'i: ... They used to say: "The 'aqila does not bear what was intentional, nor (an amount fixed by) compromise, nor a confession, nor what a slave committed, unless they accept that voluntarily."
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This ruling states that if the offender and the victim's family reach a compromise (sulh) for a specific amount, the 'aqila is not obligated to pay that amount. The proof is the same as before: the Madinan jurists listed 'bi sulh' among the things the 'aqila does not bear unless they voluntarily agree. The 'aqila is only liable for the standard diyah in accidental cases, not for any private settlement that may exceed or deviate from the norm. The sulh is between the offender and the victim's family; the 'aqila is not a party to it.

Key Terms
صُلْح (ṣulḥ): A compromise or settlement between the offender and the victim's family regarding compensation.
عَاقِلَة (ʿāqila): The paternal relatives of the offender who are collectively responsible for paying blood money (diyah) in cases of accidental killing or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, if the amount is reasonable
B) No, unless they voluntarily agree
C) Yes, always
D) Only if the qadi approves

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila is not bound by sulh agreements unless they voluntarily consent; this protects them from inflated compensation demands.

Free Person Kills Slave by Mistake: Diyah on 'Aqila
Ruling with proof from a sahabi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for accidentally killing a slave and explain that the 'aqila pays the slave's value, not the full diyah.

Core Idea
The 'aqila bears compensation for accidental killing of a slave, just as for a free person.

This lesson covers a specific case in Hanafi fiqh: when a free person accidentally kills a slave. The ruling establishes that the killer's 'aqila (tribal surety) is liable for the slave's market value, analogized from the diyah of a free person. We will examine the textual proof and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is a free person (hurr) accidentally killing a slave. The ruling is that the compensation is on the killer's 'aqila.
The act is accidental killing (qatl khata').
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? A saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab (qawl sahabi) recorded in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
The dalil is a qawl sahabi, which is a source of law in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? Umar said: 'The 'aql of a slave is in his price, just like the 'aql of a free person is in his diyah.' This analogy shows that both compensations are on the 'aqila.
The word 'mithl' indicates analogy.
4
Note the amount of compensation
The compensation is the slave's market value, not the full diyah of a free person. This is because a slave is property.
The usul note in Urdu clarifies that the slave's life is protected, but the payment is based on value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا جَنَى اخْرُّ عَلَى الْعَبْدِ فَقَتَلَهُ خَطَأَ كَانَتْ جِنَايَتُهُ عَلَى عَاقِلَتِهِ
وجه: (١) قول الصحابي لثبوت وَإِذَا جَنَى الْخُرُّ عَلَى الْعَبْدِ فَقَتَلَهُ عَنْ عُمَرَ بْنِ الْخَطَّابِ قَالَ: «وَعَقْلُ الْعَبْدِ فِي ثَمَنِهِ ، مِثْلُ عَقْلِ الخَرِ فِي دِيَتِهِ»
اصول : غلام جان آزاد کی طرح ہے، اسلئے اس کے قتل پر دیت لازم ہونی چاہئے، لیکن غلام کی قیمت دیت کی جگہ پر ہوگی۔
And if a free person commits a crime against a slave and kills him by mistake, his crime is upon his 'aqila.
Proof: Saying of a Sahabi: From 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, he said: "The 'aql (blood money) of a slave is in his price, just like the 'aql of a free person is in his diyah."
Principle (Usul): A slave is like a free person in terms of life, so diyah is obligatory for his killing, but the slave's price takes the place of diyah.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This ruling states that if a free person (hurr) accidentally kills a slave, the liability falls on the free person's 'aqila. The proof comes from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, who analogized the slave's 'aql (compensation) to the free person's diyah: just as the free person's diyah is on his 'aqila, so is the slave's value on the killer's 'aqila. The usul note in Urdu explains that a slave's life is protected, but the compensation is his value (not the full diyah of a free person). This is consistent with the Hanafi view that the 'aqila bears compensation for accidental killing, whether the victim is free or slave, but the amount differs: for a free person, it's the full diyah; for a slave, it's his market value.

Key Terms
حُرّ (ḥurr): A free person, as opposed to a slave.
عَقْل الْعَبْد ('aql al-'abd): The compensation for killing a slave, which is his market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The full diyah of a free person, paid by the killer alone
B) The slave's value, paid by the killer's 'aqila
C) The slave's value, paid by the killer alone
D) No compensation, as slaves are property

Key Takeaway
A free person's accidental killing of a slave obligates the 'aqila to pay the slave's market value, analogized from free person's diyah.

Procedure when Defendant Denies: Evidence Then Oath
Ruling with proofs for seeking evidence and oath in claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sequence of evidence and oath in Hanafi litigation and apply Abu Hanifa's view when the plaintiff claims to have evidence but demands the oath.

Core Idea
Evidence first, oath second: the plaintiff must present proof before demanding the defendant's oath.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Quduri's Kitab al-Da'wa lays out the fundamental procedure when a defendant denies a claim. The judge must first ask the plaintiff for evidence. Only if the plaintiff cannot produce evidence may he request the defendant's oath. The passage then introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute: what if the plaintiff says he has evidence but still asks for the oath? Abu Hanifa insists the evidence must be produced first, while the Sahibayn allow the oath to proceed. This lesson will guide you through the ruling, its proofs, and the practical difference between the two positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic sequence
Read the first part of the matn: when the defendant denies, the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence. If evidence is presented, judgment is given; if not, the defendant is asked to take an oath at the plaintiff's request.
This is the default Hanafi procedure.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
The commentary adds a scenario: the plaintiff says 'I have evidence present' but still demands the oath. Under Abu Hanifa, the judge must not administer the oath until the plaintiff produces his evidence. The Sahibayn differ.
The text explicitly attributes the ruling to Abu Hanifa, implying it is the relied-upon view.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
The first proof is the verse of witnesses (2:282), which commands calling two male witnesses or one male and two females. This shows that evidence (bayyinah) is the primary means to prove claims.
The verse implies that the plaintiff must first establish his claim through witnesses.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The second proof is the hadith of al-Hadrami and al-Kindi, where the Prophet asked al-Hadrami for evidence. When he had none, the Prophet said, 'Then you have his oath.' This demonstrates the sequence: evidence first, then oath.
The hadith is narrated by al-Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Compare the two views: under Abu Hanifa, a plaintiff who claims to have evidence must produce it before the defendant is sworn. Under the Sahibayn, the defendant would be sworn immediately. This affects the pace of litigation and the judge's instruction.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view as indicated by the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ أَنْكَرَ سَأَلَ الْمُدَّعِي الْبَيِّنَةَ فَإِنْ أَحْضَرَهَا قُضِيَ بِمَا ادَّعَى وَإِنْ عَجَزَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ وَطَلَبَ يَمِينَ خَصْمِهِ اُسْتُحْلِفَ عَلَيْهَا. وَإِنْ قَالَ: لِي بَيِّنَةٌ حَاضِرَةٌ وَطَلَبَ الْيَمِينَ لَمْ يُسْتَحْلَفْ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ. وَجْهٌ (١) الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ "وَإِنْ أَنْكَرَ سَأَلَ الْمُدَّعِي الْبَيِّنَةَ": ﴿وَاسْتَشْهِدُوا شَهِيدَيْنِ مِن رِّجَالِكُمْ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٨٢] إِلَى قَوْلِهِ ﴿الْأُخْرَى﴾. وَجْهٌ (٢) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ: قَالَ الْحَضْرَمِيُّ: يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ، إِنَّ هَذَا غَلَبَنِي عَلَى أَرْضٍ لِي. فَقَالَ الْكِنْدِيُّ: هِيَ أَرْضِي وَفِي يَدِي. فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ لِلْحَضْرَمِيِّ: «أَلَكَ بَيِّنَةٌ؟» قَالَ: لَا. قَالَ: «فَلَكَ يَمِينُهُ؟». (رَوَاهُ التِّرْمِذِيُّ وَأَبُو دَاوُدَ).
And if he denies, the plaintiff is asked for evidence; if he presents it, judgment is given for his claim. And if he fails in that and asks for the oath of his opponent, he is made to take an oath upon it. And if he says: 'I have evidence present' and asks for the oath, he is not made to take an oath according to Abu Hanifa. Proof (1): the verse for establishing 'If he denies, the plaintiff is asked for evidence': 'And call to witness two witnesses from among your men' up to 'the other'. Proof (2): the hadith for establishing that: Al-Hadrami said: O Messenger of Allah, this man has overcome me over my land. Al-Kindi said: It is my land and in my hand. The Prophet said to Al-Hadrami: 'Do you have evidence?' He said: No. He said: 'Then you have his oath.'
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof passage from the commentary on Al-Quduri's Kitab al-Da'wa. The matn states the procedure: when a defendant denies a claim, the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence (bayyinah). If the plaintiff produces evidence, judgment is given accordingly. If he cannot produce evidence and asks for the defendant's oath, the defendant is required to take an oath. The commentary then adds a point of ikhtilaf: if the plaintiff says 'I have evidence present' but still asks for the oath, Abu Hanifa rules that the defendant is not made to take an oath until the plaintiff produces his evidence. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the plaintiff can still demand the oath. The passage then provides two proofs: first, the Quranic verse (2:282) commanding written contracts and witnesses, which implies evidence is required; second, the famous hadith of Al-Hadrami and Al-Kindi, where the Prophet asked for evidence first, and when the plaintiff had none, offered the defendant's oath. This shows the sequence: evidence first, then oath.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, generally two male witnesses or one male and two females in property cases; also includes any proof that establishes a claim.
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn by the defendant to deny the claim; it is a decisive procedural act in litigation when the plaintiff lacks evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a plaintiff says 'I have evidence present' but asks for the defendant's oath, what should the judge do?
A) Administer the oath to the defendant immediately.
B) Require the plaintiff to produce the evidence before considering the oath.
C) Dismiss the case because the plaintiff is contradicting himself.
D) Allow the plaintiff to choose between evidence and oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi litigation, evidence must be exhausted before the defendant's oath is administered; if the plaintiff claims to have evidence, he must present it first.

8 min
The Oath Is Not Returned to the Plaintiff (La Yaruddu al-Yamin)
Ruling with proofs that the oath is not returned to the plaintiff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on radd al-yamin, cite the primary hadith evidence, and explain why the oath is not returned to the plaintiff except in qasamah.

Core Idea
The oath belongs to the defendant; refusal is admission — the oath is never returned except in qasamah.

In litigation, when the plaintiff fails to produce evidence, the defendant is required to take an oath. But what if the defendant refuses? The Hanafi school holds that the oath is not transferred back to the plaintiff; instead, the defendant's refusal is treated as an admission (nukul). This ruling is grounded in authentic hadith and is the mufta bihi position, though it differs from the Shafi'i school and even the Sahibayn within Hanafism.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Begin by clearly stating the ruling: 'The oath is not returned to the plaintiff.' This is the opening of the passage and the core legal principle.
The ruling is absolute except for the special case of qasamah.
2
Identify the primary dalil
Locate the first hadith cited from Abu Hurairah: 'al-bayyinatu 'ala man idda'a wa al-yaminu 'ala man ankara illa fi al-qasamah.' Note that it is a hadith with multiple chains.
This hadith is the foundation of the Hanafi position.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Focus on the phrase 'al-yaminu 'ala man ankara.' The oath is placed upon the denier, not upon the claimant. The exception for qasamah confirms the general rule.
The explicit assignment of the oath to the defendant prevents its return.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and the Hanafi response
The passage mentions the Shafi'i evidence (hadith of Ibn 'Abbas) and the Sahibayn's difference. Understand that the Hanafi school dismisses the return of the oath by interpreting those proofs differently (e.g., 'yamin ma'a shahid' applies only when the plaintiff has one witness).
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: if the defendant refuses, Hanafi judges rule against him; Shafi'i judges return the oath to the plaintiff.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا يَرُدُّ الْيَمِينَ عَلَى الْمُدَّعِي. وَجْهٌ (١) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ: عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ قَالَ: «الْبَيِّنَةُ عَلَى مَنِ ادَّعَى وَالْيَمِينُ عَلَى مَنْ أَنْكَرَ إِلَّا فِي الْقَسَامَةِ». وَجْهٌ (٢) عَنْ عَمْرِو بْنِ شُعَيْبٍ عَنْ أَبِيهِ عَنْ جَدِّهِ مَرْفُوعاً: «الْبَيِّنَةُ عَلَى الْمُدَّعِي وَالْيَمِينُ عَلَى الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ». وَجْهٌ (٣) عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ قَضَى بِالْيَمِينِ عَلَى الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ. وَذَكَرَ دَلِيلَ الشَّافِعِيِّ: حَدِيثَ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ «قَضَى بِيَمِينٍ وَشَاهِدٍ» وَقَوْلَ عَلِيٍّ وَابْنِ سِيرِينَ. الْأَصْلُ: مَدَّعِي عَلَيْهِ پَر قَسَم اسْوَقْت ہے جب مَدَّعِي کا گواه نہ ہو یا گواه کو پیش کرنا نا ممکن ہو، برخلاف صاحبین کے۔
The oath is not returned to the plaintiff. Proof (1): the hadith for establishing that: from Abu Hurairah that the Prophet said: 'Evidence is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the denier, except in qasamah.' Proof (2): from Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather, marfu': 'Evidence is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the defendant.' Proof (3): from Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet judged with the oath upon the defendant. And he mentioned the evidence of al-Shafi'i: the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas 'He judged with an oath and a witness' and the saying of 'Ali and Ibn Sirin. The principle: The oath is upon the defendant when the plaintiff has no witness or it is impossible to produce the witness, contrary to the Sahibayn.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage establishes the principle that in Hanafi law, the oath is not returned to the plaintiff. The ruling is that when the defendant denies, the plaintiff bears the burden of proof; if he has no evidence, the defendant takes an oath. If the defendant refuses the oath, the oath is not transferred to the plaintiff but rather the defendant's refusal is taken as an admission (nukul). The text provides three hadith proofs: (1) the well-known hadith from Abu Hurairah that the oath is on the denier, with the exception of qasamah (a special procedure in murder cases); (2) the hadith of Amr ibn Shu'ayb; and (3) the hadith that the Prophet decided with the oath on the defendant. The commentator then presents the evidence of Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that the oath can be returned to the plaintiff if the defendant refuses (based on the hadith 'judgment with an oath and a witness' and the practice of 'Ali and Shuraih). The text concludes with an Urdu principle summarizing the Hanafi position: the oath is on the defendant only when the plaintiff has no witness or cannot produce one, contrary to the Sahibayn (who allow the oath to be returned in some cases).

Key Terms
رد اليمين (radd al-yamin): Returning the oath to the plaintiff after the defendant refuses to swear; a procedure rejected by the Hanafi school (except in qasamah).
قسامة (qasamah): A special oath procedure in murder cases where the oath may be returned to the plaintiff repeatedly; the only exception to the general rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary hadith evidence for the Hanafi rule that the oath is not returned to the plaintiff?
A) Hadith of Ibn 'Abbas: 'qada bi yamin wa shahid'
B) Hadith of Abu Hurairah: 'al-yaminu 'ala man ankara'
C) Hadith of 'Ali: 'al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'i'
D) Hadith of Shuraih: 'al-yaminu 'ala man ankara illa fi al-qasamah'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the oath is not returned to the plaintiff; the defendant's refusal is treated as admission, establishing judgment against the defendant.

The Judge's Procedure for Offering Oath and Ruling on Nukul
Ruling on judge's oath procedure and nukul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for offering the oath to the defendant three times and explain the consequence of refusal (nukul).

Core Idea
The judge must offer the oath three times; refusal leads to judgment.

In Hanafi qada, when a defendant denies a claim, the judge follows a clear procedure to ensure fairness. This passage from Al-Quduri prescribes that the judge must present the oath to the defendant three times. Only after three refusals (nukul) may the judge rule against the defendant. This prevents hasty judgments and gives the defendant every opportunity to swear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action and its ruling
The action is the judge's offering of the oath to the defendant. The ruling (hukm) is that it should be done three times—'yanbaghi' indicates a strong recommendation (sunnah muakkadah).
The verb 'yanbaghi' in Hanafi fiqh often denotes a recommended procedure.
2
Understand the consequence of refusal
If the defendant refuses after three presentations, the judge passes judgment against him. This is called 'nukul'.
Nukul is a legal basis for judgment; the defendant's refusal is treated as an admission of the claim.
3
Note the repetition requirement
The judge must repeat the offer three distinct times. A single offer is insufficient; the defendant must be given multiple chances to swear.
The text explicitly says 'ثلاث مرات' (three times).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الدَّعْوَى
وَيَنْبَغِي لِلْقَاضِي أَنْ يَقُولَ لَهُ: إِنِّي أَعْرِضُ عَلَيْكَ الْيَمِينَ ثَلَاثًا فَإِنْ حَلَفْت وَإِلَّا قَضَيْت عَلَيْكَ مَا ادَّعَاهُ فَإِذَا كَرَّرَ عَلَيْهِ الْعَرْضَ ثَلَاثَ مَرَّاتٍ قَضَى عَلَيْهِ بِالنُّكُولِ
It is befitting for the judge to say to him: 'I present the oath to you three times. If you swear, [fine]; otherwise, I will judge against you what is claimed.' So when he repeats the presentation three times, he judges against him due to refusal (nukul).
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the procedure of oath in claims. The Hanafi judge must offer the oath three times to the defendant. If the defendant swears, he is free; if he refuses after three presentations, the judge passes judgment against him. This is the general rule for most claims.

Key Terms
نُكُول (nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take the oath after being offered by the judge, which leads to judgment against him in Hanafi fiqh.
يَمِين (yamīn): Oath sworn by the defendant to deny the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Once
B) Twice
C) Three times
D) Continuously until he swears

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi judge must offer the oath three times before ruling on refusal (nukul).

Oath in Claims of Marriage and Lineage: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on the exception of oath in claims of marriage, lineage, and similar matters, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding oath administration in marriage and similar claims, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa exempts marriage and similar claims from oath; the Sahibayn require it except in hudud and li'an.

This passage from Al-Quduri presents a key procedural dispute within the Hanafi school. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples differ on whether a defendant must be offered an oath in claims concerning marriage, lineage, and other matters. Understanding this dispute reveals how the school balances textual rulings and practical rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims listed
Read the source passage and list all the claims mentioned: marriage (nikah), return (رجعة), fay' in ila', slavery, istilad, wala', lineage, hudud, and li'an.
Li'an is mentioned separately as an exception in the Sahibayn's view.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
According to Abu Hanifa, in all these claims (including hudud), no oath is administered to the defendant. The judge does not offer an oath.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that oath is administered in all these claims except hudud and li'an. In hudud and li'an, no oath is given for specific reasons.
Li'an has a separate procedure; hudud are based on strong evidentiary standards.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in a marriage claim, according to Abu Hanifa the defendant is never asked to swear; according to the Sahibayn, the judge must offer the oath. This affects the outcome of the case.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of the Sahibayn: oath is administered in all claims except hudud and li'an. This is because it better protects rights and prevents false denials.
The tarjih is based on the later preference of the majority of Hanafi jurists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا كَانَتْ الدَّعْوَى نِكَاحًا لَمْ يُسْتَحْلَفْ الْمُنْكِرُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ ١٣ وَلَا يُسْتَخْلَفُ فِي النِّكَاحِ وَالرَّجْعَةِ وَالْفَيْءِ فِي الْإِيلَاءِ وَالرّقِ وَالِاسْتِيلادِ وَالْوَلَاءِ وَالنَّسَبِ وَالخُدُودِ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ: يُسْتَحْلَفُ فِي ذَلِكَ كُلِّهِ إِلَّا فِي الْحُدُودِ وَاللَّعَانِ
And when the claim is marriage, the denier is not made to swear according to Abu Hanifa. And oath is not administered in marriage, return (رجعة), fay' in ila', slavery, istilad, wala', lineage, and hudud. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: oath is administered in all of that except hudud and li'an.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the defendant must be offered an oath in certain types of claims. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, in claims involving marriage, reconciliation after divorce (رجعة), return to conjugal relations in ila' (فَيْء), status of slavery, motherhood (استيلاد), clientage (ولاء), lineage, and fixed punishments (حدود), no oath is administered to the defendant. Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) differ: they hold that oath is required in all such claims except hudud and li'an. The practical effect is significant in court procedure.

Key Terms
اسْتِيلاد (istīlād): The status of a slave woman becoming the mother of her master's child (umm walad).
وَلَاء (walā'): Right of patronage or clientage arising from manumission.
لِعَان (li'ān): Mutual imprecation between spouses when husband accuses wife of adultery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which claim does Abu Hanifa say oath is not administered?
A) Claims of debt
B) Claims of marriage
C) Claims of sale
D) Claims of damage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi practice, oath is administered in claims of marriage, return, and similar matters except hudud and li'an, per the relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Two Claimants for a Specific Thing in Possession of a Third Party
Ruling on two claimants with evidence for a specific thing in the hand of a single third party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants each present evidence for the same specific item held by a third party and explain the rationale of equal division.

Core Idea
When both claimants have equal evidence, the item is split equally — the judge cannot prefer one over the other.

This lesson addresses a classic scenario in qada (judicial procedure): two separate individuals both claim ownership of a specific item, but the item is in the hands of a third person. Each claimant has evidence. How does the judge decide? The Hanafi ruling provides a clear and equitable solution based on the strength of equal evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the object
Two claimants assert ownership of a specific tangible thing ('ayn) that is in the possession of a single third party (the defendant).
2
Recognize the condition
Both claimants present valid evidence (bayyinah). Without evidence, the case would be different.
3
Understand the ruling
The judge divides the thing equally between the two claimants (yuqda bima baynahuma).
Division is equal as if each has proven half ownership.
4
Appreciate the principle
When evidence is equally strong, neither claimant is preferred. Equal division is the only just outcome to avoid bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا ادَّعَى اثْنَانِ عَيْنًا فِي يَدِ رَجُلٍ وَاحِدٍ وَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ يَزْعُمُ أَنَّهَا لَهُ وَأَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ قُضِيَ بِمَا بَيْنَهُمَا
And when two persons claim a specific thing in the hand of one man, and each claims that it is his, and they bring evidence, judgment is given for what is between them (i.e., it is divided between them).
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This is a ruling for a scenario where two separate persons both claim ownership of a specific item, but the item is not in the possession of either claimant; rather it is in the possession of a third party (the defendant). Both claimants each have their own evidence. In such a case, the judge rules that the item is divided equally between the two claimants, as if each has proven half ownership.

Key Terms
عَيْن ('ayn): A specific tangible thing (as opposed to a debt or right).
بَيِّنَة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When two claimants each bring evidence for the same specific item in a third party's hand, how does the judge decide?
A) He dismisses both claims
B) He gives it to the one with stronger evidence
C) He divides it equally between them
D) He gives it to the third party

Key Takeaway
When two claimants bring evidence for a specific thing in a third party's hand, the Hanafi judge divides it equally between them.

Two Claimants for Marriage of the Same Woman
Ruling on two claimants for marriage to the same woman, both with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when two men claim marriage to the same woman and both present evidence, and explain why the woman's confirmation is decisive.

Core Idea
When evidence conflicts, marriage is decided by the woman's own confirmation, not by the judge's preference.

In this ruling, we encounter a conflict of evidence in a marriage claim. Two men each present proof that they married the same woman. Rather than weigh the evidence, the judge refers the matter to the woman herself. This underscores the personal nature of marriage and the woman's authoritative knowledge of her own marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Two men both claim to have married the same woman, and each brings evidence (bayyinah) to support his claim.
The evidence could be witnesses or documents.
2
Identify the judge's default approach
Normally, a judge would weigh evidence to determine which claim is stronger. However, in this case, the judge does not prefer either evidence.
3
State the ruling
The ruling is that the judge does not give judgment based on either evidence, but instead turns to the woman's confirmation (tasdiq) of one of the claimants.
The Arabic says 'رَجَعَ إِلَى تَصْدِيقِ الْمَرْأَةِ' (reverts to the woman's confirmation).
4
Explain the rationale
Marriage is a personal status that the woman herself knows best. Her confirmation or denial settles the conflict because it involves her consent and knowledge.
This principle respects the woman's autonomy and the need for her explicit acknowledgment in marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ ادَّعَى كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا نِكَاحَ امْرَأَةٍ وَأَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ لَمْ يُقْضَ بِوَاحِدَةٍ مِنْ الْبَيِّنَتَيْنِ وَرَجَعَ إِلَى تَصْدِيقِ الْمَرْأَةِ لِأَحَدِهِمَا
And if each of the two claims marriage to a woman and they bring evidence, judgment is not given for either of the two evidences, and it reverts to the woman's confirmation of one of them.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
In this ruling, two men both claim to have married the same woman, and each brings evidence to prove his marriage. The judge does not validate either evidence. Instead, the woman is asked to confirm which one is her husband. Her testimony decides the case, because marriage involves her personal status and consent.

Key Terms
تَصْدِيق (taṣdīq): Confirmation or acknowledgment by the woman as to which claimant is her husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The judge marries her to both
C) The woman's confirmation decides
D) The judge dismisses both

Key Takeaway
When two men both claim marriage to the same woman with evidence, the decisive factor is the woman's own confirmation, not the evidence.

Two Claimants for Purchase: Rules of Preference
Rulings on two claimants for purchase from one seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when two claimants each claim purchase of the same item from the same seller, and apply the preference hierarchy (date, possession, claim type) to determine who gets the item.

Core Idea
When two claimants each claim to have bought the same item, they may take half; if a date exists, the earlier buyer wins; otherwise possession or type of claim decides.

When two people each claim to have purchased the same item from the same seller, their competing claims must be resolved through a clear set of rules. This passage from Quduri outlines the Hanafi approach: initially each claimant has an option to take half; if that fails, preferences based on date, possession, or the nature of the claim apply. Mastering these rules is essential for any judge or scholar dealing with disputes over ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
There are two claimants, each asserting they bought the same slave from the same seller. Both produce evidence (bayyinah).
2
Apply the initial option rule
Each claimant has the option to take half the slave for half the price or to abandon the claim. If a judge has already ruled on the division and one claimant says "I do not choose," the other cannot take the entire slave.
The option is personal and does not transfer upon refusal.
3
Preference based on date
If both claimants mention a date of purchase (tārīkh), the one with the earlier date is preferred and gets the slave.
4
Preference based on possession
If neither provides a date but one has actual possession (qabḍ), that person is preferred.
Possession indicates a stronger claim in the absence of a date.
5
Competing claims: purchase vs. gift or marriage
If one claims purchase and the other claims a gift with possession, the purchase claim prevails. If one claims purchase and a woman claims she was married on consideration of the slave (i.e., the slave was her mahr), then the two claims are treated equally.
Sale is a stronger basis for ownership than gift; marriage claim is seen as equivalent to purchase when no date or possession distinguishes them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ ادَّعَى اثْنَانِ عَلَى رَجُل كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا يَزْعُمُ أَنَّهُ اشْتَرَى مِنْهُ هَذَا الْعَبْدَ وَأَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ فَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَ نِصْفَ الْعَبْدِ بِنِصْفِ الثَّمَنِ ل وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَ فَإِنْ قَضَى بِهِ الْقَاضِي بَيْنَهُمَا فَقَالَ أَحَدُهُمَا: لَا أَخْتَارُ لَمْ يَكُنْ لِلْآخَرِ أَنْ يَأْخُذَ جَمِيعَهُ ١٨ وَلَوْ ذَكَرَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا تَارِيحًا فَهُوَ لِلْأَوَّلِ مِنْهُمَا 19 وَإِنْ لَمْ يَذْكُرَا تَارِيحًا وَمَعَ أَحَدِهِمَا قَبْضٌ فَهُوَ أَوْلَى ٢٠ فَإِنْ ادَّعَى أَحَدُهُمَا شِرَاءً وَالْآخَرُ هِبَةَ وَقَبْضًا وَإِنْ أَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ وَلَا تَارِيخَ مَعَهُمَا فَالشِّرَاءُ أَوْلَى وَإِنْ ادَّعَى أَحَدُهُمَا الشَّرَاءَ وَادَّعَتْ امْرَأَةٌ أَنَّهُ تَزَوَّجَهَا عَلَيْهِ فَهُمَا سَوَاءٌ
And if two persons claim against a man, each claiming that he bought this slave from him, and they bring evidence, then each of them has the option: if he wishes, he takes half the slave for half the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. And if the judge rules it between them, then one of them says: I do not choose, it is not for the other to take all of it. And if each of them mentions a date, then it is for the earlier of them. And if they do not mention a date and one of them has possession, then he is preferred. Then if one of them claims purchase and the other claims a gift with possession, and they bring evidence and they have no date, then purchase is preferred. And if one claims purchase and a woman claims that he married her on it (i.e., on the slave), then they are equal.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage contains several interconnected rulings for a complex claim scenario. Two men each claim to have bought the same slave from a single seller (the defendant). Both have evidence. Here, each claimant has an option: he may either take half of the slave by paying half the price, or abandon his claim. If the judge has already given a ruling dividing the slave between them, and one claimant says he does not choose to take half, then the other claimant cannot take the entire slave because the first claimant's right is still there? Actually the text says 'fain qada bihi al-qadi baynahuma faqala ahaduhuma: la akhtaru lam yakun lil-akhari an yakhudha jami'ahu' meaning if the judge adjudicates the slave between them and one says 'I do not choose', the other cannot take all of it. That seems to mean the option is not transferable. Then if each mentions a date of purchase, the one with the earlier date gets the slave. If no date, but one has actual possession (qabd), he is preferred. If one claims purchase and the other claims a gift with possession, purchase is preferred over gift because sale is a stronger cause of ownership. Finally, if one claims purchase and a woman claims he married her on the slave (i.e., the slave was part of an agreement or something?), they are equal; this likely means the claims are treated equally and the slave is divided? Or maybe they both have equal footing, and the judge must seek further evidence? The text says 'huma sawa' meaning they are equal.

Key Terms
خِيار (khiyār): Option or choice given to each claimant to take half or leave.
تَارِيخ (tārīkh): Date of the contract, used to determine priority based on earlier date.
قَبْض (qabḍ): Physical possession, which gives preference in case of no date.
بَيِّنَة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically witness testimony or documentary proof.
هِبَة (hibah): Gift, a transfer of ownership without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two claimants for purchase of the same slave from the same seller have no date, but one has possession, who is preferred?
A) The one with possession
B) The one with earlier date
C) They are equal
D) The judge gives it to the seller

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when two claimants each claim purchase of the same item, preferences are determined in order: earlier date, then possession, then type of claim (purchase over gift, but equal with marriage).

Qada — Judiciary
beginner
6 min
Dividing the Object and Marriage Claim Exception
Primary ruling and its marriage exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule when two claimants present equal evidence for a physical object held by a third party, and distinguish the marriage claim exception.

Core Idea
Equal evidence for property leads to division; for marriage, the woman decides.

When two people claim the same object and both have credible witnesses, the judge faces a dilemma. The Hanafi school, following prophetic practice, divides the object equally between them. But the same logic does not apply when both claim marriage to the same woman—her testimony breaks the tie. In this lesson, you will learn the reason for this distinction and its evidentiary basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First scenario: two claimants for a physical object (ayn) in the hand of a third party. Second scenario: two men claiming marriage to the same woman.
The first involves property, the second involves personal status.
2
Contrast the rulings
For the object, evidence is accepted and the object is divided equally. For marriage, both pieces of evidence are rejected, and the woman's confirmation decides.
Why the difference? The woman's body is not divisible, and her consent is paramount.
3
Examine the prophetic hadith
The hadith from Abu Dawood reports that the Prophet (ﷺ) divided a camel equally between two claimants who each brought two witnesses.
This is the primary proof for the division rule.
4
Understand the marriage exception's rationale
In marriage, the woman's testimony is given weight because the right to marry is personal and cannot be split. The evidence is set aside to let her choose.
This is a special rule based on the nature of marriage, not a general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الدَّعْوَى
وَإِذَا ادَّعَى اثْنَانِ عَيْنًا فِي يَدِ رَجُلٍ وَاحِدٍ وَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ يَزْعُمُ أَنَّهَا لَهُ وَأَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ قُضِيَ بِمَا بَيْنَهُمَا
وَإِنْ ادَّعَى كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا نِكَاحَ امْرَأَةٍ وَأَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ لَمْ يُقْضَ بِوَاحِدَةٍ مِنَ الْبَيِّنَتَيْنِ وَرَجَعَ إِلَى تَصْدِيقِ الْمَرْأَةِ لِأَحَدِهِمَا
وَإِنْ ادَّعَى اثْنَانِ عَلَى رَجُلٍ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا يَزْعُمُ أَنَّهُ اشْتَرَى مِنْهُ هَذَا الْعَبْدَ وَأَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ فَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَ نِصْفَ الْعَبْدِ بِنِصْفِ الثَّمَنِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَ فَإِنْ قَضَى بِهِ الْقَاضِي بَيْنَهُمَا فَقَالَ أَحَدُهُمَا: لَا أَخْتَارُ لَمْ يَكُنْ لِلْآخَرِ أَنْ يَأْخُذَ جَمِيعَهُ
وَلَوْ ذَكَرَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا تَارِيخًا فَهُوَ لِلْأَوَّلِ مِنْهُمَا
وَإِنْ لَمْ يَذْكُرَا تَارِيخًا وَمَعَ أَحَدِهِمَا قَبْضٌ فَهُوَ أَوْلَى
فَإِنِ ادَّعَى أَحَدُهُمَا شِرَاءً وَالْآخَرُ هِبَةً وَقَبْضًا وَإِنْ أَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ وَلَا تَارِيخَ مَعَهُمَا فَالشِّرَاءُ أَوْلَى
وَإِنِ ادَّعَى أَحَدُهُمَا الشَّرَاءَ وَادَّعَتِ امْرَأَةٌ أَنَّهُ تَزَوَّجَهَا عَلَيْهِ فَهُمَا سَوَاءٌ
وَإِنِ ادَّعَى أَحَدُهُمَا رَهْنًا وَقَبْضًا وَالْآخَرُ هِبَةً وَقَبْضًا فَالرَّهْنُ أَوْلَى مِنَ الْهِبَةِ
وَإِنْ أَقَامَ الْخَارِجَانِ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الْمِلْكِ وَالتَّارِيخِ فَصَاحِبُ التَّارِيخِ الْأَبْعَدِ أَوْلَى
فَإِنِ ادَّعَيَا الشِّرَاءَ مِنْ وَاحِدٍ وَأَقَامَا الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى تَارِيخَيْنِ فَالْأَوَّلُ أَوْلَى
وَإِنْ أَقَامَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بَيِّنَةً عَلَى الشِّرَاءِ مِنْ آخَرَ وَذَكَرَا تَارِيخًا فَهُمَا سَوَاءٌ
وَإِنْ أَقَامَ الْخَارِجُ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى مِلْكٍ مُؤَرَّخٍ وَأَقَامَ صَاحِبُ الْيَدِ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى مِلْكٍ أَقْدَمَ تَارِيخًا كَانَ أَوْلَى
ثُمَّ ذَكَرَ الْمُصَنِّفُ الْأَحَادِيثَ:
وجه (١): الحديث لثبوت "وَإِذَا ادَّعَى اثْنَانِ عَيْنًا فِي يَدِ رَجُلٍ وَاحِدٍ" عَنْ قَتَادَةَ بِمَعْنَى إِسْنَادِهِ أَنَّ رَجُلَيْنِ ادَّعَيَا بَعِيرًا عَلَى عَهْدِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَبَعَثَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا شَاهِدَيْنِ فَقَسَمَهُ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَيْنِ (سنن أبي داود، باب الرجلين يدعيان شيئًا وليست لهما بينة، رقم 3615)
وجه (٢): الحديث لثبوت "وَإِنِ ادَّعَى اثْنَانِ عَلَى رَجُلٍ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا" عَنْ قَتَادَةَ بِمَعْنَى إِسْنَادِهِ أَنَّ رَجُلَيْنِ ادَّعَيَا بَعِيرًا عَلَى عَهْدِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَبَعَثَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا شَاهِدَيْنِ فَقَسَمَهُ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَيْنِ (سنن أبي داود، باب الرجلين يدعيان شيئًا وليست لهما بينة، رقم 3615)
وجه (٣): الحديث لثبوت "وَلَوْ ذَكَرَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا تَارِيخًا" عَنْ سَمُرَةَ بْنِ جُنْدَبٍ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «إِذَا بِيعَ الْبَيْعُ مِنْ رَجُلَيْنِ فَالْبَيْعُ لِلْأَوَّلِ» (سنن ابن ماجه، باب من اشترط الخلاص، رقم 2344)
وجه (٤): الحديث لثبوت "وَإِنْ لَمْ يَذْكُرَا تَارِيخًا وَمَعَ أَحَدِهِمَا قَبْضٌ فَهُوَ أَوْلَى" عَنْ جَابِرِ بْنِ عَبْدِ اللهِ أَنَّ رَجُلَيْنِ تَدَاعَيَا بِدَابَّةٍ فَأَقَامَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا الْبَيِّنَةَ أَنَّهَا دَابَّتُهُ نَتَجَهَا فَقَضَى بِهَا رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم لِلَّذِي هِيَ فِي يَدَيْهِ (السنن الكبرى للبيهقي، باب المتداعيين يتنازعان شيئًا في يد أحدهما ويقيم كل واحد منهما على ذلك بينة، رقم 21223)
وجه (٥): الحديث لثبوت "وَإِنِ ادَّعَى أَحَدُهُمَا رَهْنًا وَقَبْضًا وَالْآخَرُ هِبَةً وَقَبْضًا" عَنْ سَمُرَةَ بْنِ جُنْدَبٍ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «إِذَا بِيعَ الْبَيْعُ مِنْ رَجُلَيْنِ فَالْبَيْعُ لِلْأَوَّلِ» (سنن ابن ماجه، باب من اشترط الخلاص، رقم 2344)
وجه (٦): الحديث لثبوت "فَإِنِ ادَّعَيَا الشِّرَاءَ مِنْ وَاحِدٍ" عَنْ أَسْمَرَ بْنِ مُضَرِّسٍ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَنْ سُبِقَ إِلَى مَا لَمْ يَسْبِقْهُ إِلَيْهِ مُسْلِمٌ فَهُوَ لَهُ» يُرِيدُ بِهِ إِحْيَاءَ الْمَوَاتِ (السنن الكبرى للبيهقي، باب القاضي يقدم الناس الأول فالأول فللأول حق السبق والسبق أصل في الشريعة، رقم 20484)
وجه (٧): الحديث لثبوت "وَإِنْ أَقَامَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بَيِّنَةً" عَنْ قَتَادَةَ بِمَعْنَى إِسْنَادِهِ أَنَّ رَجُلَيْنِ ادَّعَيَا بَعِيرًا عَلَى عَهْدِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَبَعَثَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا شَاهِدَيْنِ فَقَسَمَهُ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَيْنِ (سنن أبي داود، باب الرجلين يدعيان شيئًا وليست لهما بينة، رقم 3615)
وجه (٨): الحديث لثبوت "وَإِنْ أَقَامَ الْخَارِجُ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى مِلْكٍ مُؤَرَّخٍ" عَنْ سَمُرَةَ بْنِ جُنْدَبٍ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «إِذَا بِيعَ الْبَيْعُ مِنْ رَجُلَيْنِ فَالْبَيْعُ لِلْأَوَّلِ» (سنن ابن ماجه، باب من اشترط الخلاص، رقم 2344)
ثُمَّ ذَكَرَ الْمُصَنِّفُ أُصُولًا:
أصول: كسي مخصوص شے کے متعلق دو مدعی ہو اور دونوں کے پاس بینہ ہو تو دونوں کے لئے درمیان فیصلہ ہو گا۔
أصول: خرید ناہبہ سے زیادہ تقویت دیتا ہے۔
أصول: مقدم تاریخ پر کسی کا دعوی نہیں ہو تو چیز اس کو دے دی جائے گی۔
Book of Claims. If two people claim an object in the hand of one man, each claiming it is his, and they present evidence, it is judged between them. If each of them claims marriage to a woman and they present evidence, neither evidence is judged and it returns to the woman's confirmation for one of them. If two people claim against a man, each claiming that he bought this slave from him, and they present evidence, then each of them has the option: if he wishes, he takes half the slave with half the price, and if he wishes, he leaves. If the judge adjudicates it between them and one of them says, 'I do not choose,' the other may not take it all. If each of them mentions a date, it is for the earlier of them. If they do not mention a date and one of them has possession, he has priority. If one claims purchase and the other claims gift with possession, and they present evidence without a date, then purchase has priority. If one claims purchase and a woman claims that he married her on it, they are equal. If one claims pledge with possession and the other claims gift with possession, then pledge has priority over gift. If two outsiders present evidence of ownership with a date, then the one with the later date has priority. If they both claim purchase from one person and present evidence with two dates, the earlier has priority. If each of them presents evidence of purchase from another and they mention a date, they are equal. If the outsider presents evidence of ownership dated and the possessor presents evidence of an earlier dated ownership, the earlier has priority. Then the author mentioned the hadith: (followed by eight hadith with citations). Then the author mentioned principles: (Urdu: If two claimants for a specific object both have evidence, the judgment is between them. Purchase is stronger than gift. If no one claims an earlier date, the object is given to him.)
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) in Al-Quduri's matn presents a series of rulings on competing claims, each supported by hadith evidence. The rulings address scenarios where two claimants present evidence for the same object or right. The first ruling: when two claimants both present evidence for an object held by a third party, the judge divides it equally between them, based on the hadith of the two men who claimed a camel and the Prophet divided it. Second: if two men claim marriage to the same woman, neither evidence is accepted; instead the woman chooses. Third: if two claimants each claim to have bought a slave from the same seller, each has the option to take half at half price; if one declines, the other cannot take the whole. Fourth: if dates are mentioned, the earlier date prevails, based on the hadith 'the sale is for the first'. Fifth: if no date but one has possession, that one is preferred, based on the hadith of Jabir. Sixth: purchase claim prevails over gift claim when both have evidence but no date. Seventh: purchase and marriage claim are equal. Eighth: pledge prevails over gift. Ninth: when outsiders present evidence, the one with the later date is preferred. Tenth: if both claim purchase from the same seller with different dates, the earlier wins. Eleventh: if each claims purchase from different sellers with dates, they are equal. Twelfth: if the outsider has a later date and the possessor an earlier date, the earlier wins. The hadith include references to Abu Dawood, Ibn Majah, and Bayhaqi, confirming the rulings. The Urdu usul notes summarize: for a specific object with two claimants and evidence, split between them; purchase is stronger than gift; and if no prior claim, give to the one with earlier date.

Key Terms
البَيِّنَة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically two witnesses, presented in court to prove a claim.
التَّصْدِيق (al-tasdīq): Confirmation or verification, here the woman's statement identifying which claimant is her husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men claim to be married to the same woman and both present two witnesses. What does the Hanafi judge do?
A) Divide the woman between them equally.
B) Accept the first evidence presented.
C) Reject both and ask the woman whom she confirms.
D) Cast lots.

Key Takeaway
When evidence is equal, divide property; for marriage, the woman's word decides.

Options and Dates in Competing Purchase Claims
Rulings on two purchasers of the same slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for two claimants who both claim to have purchased the same slave from the same seller, including the option to take half, the effect of dates, and the role of possession.

Core Idea
For two buyers of the same slave, each may take half at half price or decline; dates and possession break ties.

When two people each claim to have bought the same slave from the same seller, the judge must navigate competing contracts. The Hanafi school gives each buyer an option: accept half at half the price or walk away. If dates or possession exist, they tilt the scale. This lesson unpacks these rules with their prophetic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
Two claimants each claim purchase of the same slave from the same seller and present evidence. The judge gives each the choice to take half the slave for half the price or leave it.
This is a compromise; full price applies only if one declines.
2
Understand the effect of dates
If each mentions a date of purchase, the earlier date prevails—the slave goes entirely to the first buyer at the full price.
Date establishes priority of contract.
3
Examine the role of possession without date
If no dates are mentioned but one claimant has possession (qabd), that one is preferred.
Possession suggests a completed sale.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
Date priority is based on the hadith: 'the sale is for the first' (Ibn Majah). Possession priority is based on the hadith of Jabir: the Prophet gave the animal to the one in whose hand it was.
These are ahad hadith accepted by Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
